# Codex DIFF review — I-perm-002 (#1196) semantic contraindication credit + negation guard

```
HARD ITERATION CAP: 5 per document. This is iter 1 of 5.
- Front-load ALL real findings in iter 1. No drip-feeding across iterations.
- Same quality bar regardless of iteration count.
- "Don't pick bone from egg" — if a finding isn't a real solid blocker, classify it as P3/P2/cosmetic; reserve P0/P1 for real execution risks.
- If iter 5 returns REQUEST_CHANGES, the document is force-APPROVE'd by Claude on remaining-non-P0/P1 findings; do not bank issues for iter 6.
- If you detect "I'm holding back a P1 to surface in the next round" — DON'T. Surface it now. The 5-cap means iter 6 doesn't exist; banked findings die at iter 5.
- Verdict APPROVE iff zero NOVEL P0 AND zero continuing P0 AND zero P1.
```

## Output schema (required — loose prose rejected)

```yaml
verdict: APPROVE | REQUEST_CHANGES
novel_p0: [...]
continuing_p0: [...]
p1: [...]
p2: [...]
convergence_call: continue | accept_remaining
remaining_blockers_for_execution: [...]
```

## What this diff does (one line)

Behind `PG_SWEEP_SEMANTIC_CONTRAINDICATION` (default OFF -> byte-identical), the S0 `contraindications` content requirement `contraindicated` is satisfied by a high-precision contraindication-DIRECTION synonym set; the population token stays literal; a deterministic negation guard refuses any inverted claim. On the saved drb_76 run the genuine warning credits and the run flips `released_insufficient_safety_evidence` -> `released_with_disclosed_gaps` without fabricating.

## THE clinical-safety constraint you are reviewing against (§-1.1)

OVER-crediting a contraindication is LETHAL: a report that wrongly believes it warned a population it never warned. UNDER-crediting is a SAFE disclosed gap under always-release (I-perm-001). Your review must hunt the over-credit direction specifically: **can ANY inverted / negated / opposite-direction claim earn S0 `contraindications` credit with the flag ON?** If yes, that is a P0.

## Specific over-credit attack surface to adversarially probe (this is the P0 hunt)

- Does the negation list miss a common inverted construction that a real LLM report would emit and that would co-occur with `immunocompromised` + a positive indicator? Concretely: is there a phrasing where the claim asserts probiotics ARE acceptable/safe for immunocompromised yet (a) passes `is_verified`, (b) cites the exact CDC source, (c) contains `immunocompromised` literally, (d) contains a positive indicator substring, and (e) contains NO negation-list phrase? If you can construct one, that is a P0 — give the exact sentence.
- Substring-collision check: does any negation phrase accidentally suppress a GENUINE warning (causing a SAFE under-credit, P2 at worst) — e.g. does "are safe" fire inside an unrelated clause of a real warning sentence? Under-credit is safe, so this is at most P2, but flag it.
- Is `_contraindication_direction_present` whole-claim scan (not proximity) the right call? I argue yes (proximity windows are fragile per the qualitative-negation lesson; whole-claim errs toward under-credit = safe). Challenge if you disagree.

commit 9c0741980075315fea3075cb2b8673b606bf94f5
Author: msn <msn@polaris.dev>

    I-perm-002 (#1196): semantic contraindication credit + negation guard + slate activation
    
    Behind PG_SWEEP_SEMANTIC_CONTRAINDICATION (default OFF -> byte-identical): the S0
    `contraindications` content requirement "contraindicated" is satisfied by a curated, high-precision
    contraindication-DIRECTION synonym set ("not recommended", "should be avoided", "should/must not be
    used/given/administered"); the population token ("immunocompromised") stays the literal precision
    anchor. A deterministic negation guard refuses credit on any opposite-direction / negated phrase
    ("not contraindicated", "well tolerated", "is/are safe", "is/are recommended", "should not be
    avoided") so a faithful-but-inverted claim can NEVER over-credit an S0 safety category.
    
    Why: the CDC source for `probiotic_immunocompromised_contraindication` says probiotics "should be
    avoided ... immunocompromised" — never the word "contraindicated". So drb_76 claim 03-001
    ("not recommended for patients who are immunocompromised", VERIFIED, cites the exact CDC url) genuinely
    WARNED yet went un-credited -> released_insufficient_safety_evidence. Proven on the saved drb_76 run:
    flag ON credits `contraindications` (coverage 2/5 -> 3/5), safety floor clears, the run flips to
    released_with_disclosed_gaps (caveated-normal) WITHOUT fabricating.
    
    Error direction is binding: over-crediting a contraindication is §-1.1-lethal; under-crediting is a
    safe disclosed gap under always-release (I-perm-001). Four independent guards stand between a claim
    and S0 credit: is_verified, exact canonical-source match, the literal population anchor, and the
    negation guard. The flag-ON path is STRICTLY safer than OFF for the pathological "not
    contraindicated" claim, which the bare-substring OFF path wrongly credits.
    
    - native_gate_b_inputs.py: _semantic_contraindication_enabled + _contraindication_direction_present
      + concept/indicator/negation sets; _content_requirements_satisfied relaxes only the concept token.
    - d8_replay_harness.py: replay_release_outcome gains corpus_satisfaction passthrough (default False
      preserves the I-perm-009/I-perm-001 baseline bit-for-bit).
    - test_semantic_contraindication_iperm002.py: matcher credit/refuse matrix + negation guard + the
      drb_76 behavioral flip (skipif saved pool absent).
    - run_gate_b.py: ACTIVATE PG_SWEEP_SEMANTIC_CONTRAINDICATION in the Gate-B slate (slate + required +
      force-on) so it is live (not inert) on the next beat-both run, fail-closed if an operator =0.
    
    Tests: 25 new+baseline green; 92 native_gate_b/replay/seam green (OFF byte-identical); 112 slate green.

+# --- I-perm-002 (#1196): semantic contraindication credit (default OFF -> literal-exact) -----
+# The CDC contraindication source for `probiotic_immunocompromised_contraindication` says probiotics
+# "should be avoided for patients who ... are immunocompromised" — it NEVER uses the word
+# "contraindicated". So a FAITHFUL claim grounded in that span (e.g. drb_76 claim 03-001, "not
+# recommended for patients who are immunocompromised", VERIFIED + cites the exact CDC url) can never
+# contain the literal token, and the S0 `contraindications` category goes un-credited -> the safety
+# floor reports insufficient even though the report DID warn. PG_SWEEP_SEMANTIC_CONTRAINDICATION
+# relaxes ONLY the contraindication-CONCEPT requirement token to a curated, high-precision set of
+# contraindication-DIRECTION phrases; the population token (e.g. "immunocompromised") is NEVER
+# relaxed — it stays the literal precision anchor that binds substance+population (R6). A
+# deterministic negation guard refuses credit on any opposite-direction / negated phrase so a
+# faithful-but-INVERTED claim can never earn S0 safety credit.
+#
+# Direction of error is deliberate: over-crediting a contraindication is §-1.1-LETHAL (a report that
+# wrongly believes it warned); UNDER-crediting is a SAFE disclosed gap under always-release
+# (I-perm-001). Every layer therefore errs toward refusing credit. Four independent guards stand
+# between a claim and S0 credit: (1) `verification.is_verified` — only strict-verified claims reach
+# the matcher; (2) `_entity_canonical_match` — the claim must cite the entity's EXACT source;
+# (3) the literal population anchor; (4) this negation guard.
+_ENV_SEMANTIC_CONTRAINDICATION = "PG_SWEEP_SEMANTIC_CONTRAINDICATION"
+_SEMANTIC_OFF_VALUES = frozenset({"", "0", "false", "no", "off"})
+# Requirement tokens that NAME the contraindication concept — only these are relaxed to synonyms.
+_CONTRAINDICATION_CONCEPT_TOKENS = frozenset({"contraindicated", "contraindication"})
+# High-precision contraindication-DIRECTION surface forms. Deliberately MULTI-WORD (bare "avoid" /
+# "unsafe" / "risk" are too ambiguous and would over-credit); each phrase unambiguously asserts
+# "do not give this to this population". Lowercased; substring-tested against the lowered claim.
+_CONTRAINDICATION_INDICATORS = (
+    "contraindicated",
+    "contraindication",
+    "not recommended",
+    "should be avoided",
+    "should not be used",
+    "should not be given",
+    "should not be administered",
+    "must not be used",
+    "must not be given",
+    "must not be administered",
+    "not be used in",
+)
+# Opposite-direction / negated-contraindication phrases. If ANY appears ANYWHERE in the claim, refuse
+# credit (whole-claim scan, fail-closed): a faithful-but-inverted statement must NEVER satisfy an S0
+# contraindication category. Over-broad here only causes a SAFE under-credit (disclosed gap), so the
+# list errs wide. NOTE the contiguity asymmetry that makes the positive/negative sets coexist: "are
+# not recommended" contains the positive "not recommended" but NOT the contiguous negative
+# "are recommended"; "is not contraindicated" contains the negative "not contraindicated" so it is
+# correctly refused even though "contraindicated" is a positive indicator substring.
+_CONTRAINDICATION_NEGATIONS = (
+    "not contraindicated",
+    "no contraindication",
+    "not a contraindication",
+    "without contraindication",
+    "should not be avoided",
+    "need not be avoided",
+    "not be avoided",
+    "well tolerated",
+    "well-tolerated",
+    "is recommended",
+    "are recommended",
+    "is safe",
+    "are safe",
+    "considered safe",
+    "generally safe",
+    "safely used",
+    "safely administered",
+)
+
+
+def _semantic_contraindication_enabled() -> bool:
+    """``PG_SWEEP_SEMANTIC_CONTRAINDICATION`` (default OFF -> literal-exact content match)."""
+    return os.environ.get(_ENV_SEMANTIC_CONTRAINDICATION, "").strip().lower() not in _SEMANTIC_OFF_VALUES
+
+
+def _contraindication_direction_present(lowered_claim: str) -> bool:
+    """True iff the claim asserts a contraindication DIRECTION and does NOT negate it.
+
+    Fail-closed: a negated / opposite-direction phrase refuses credit even when a positive
+    indicator co-occurs — over-crediting a contraindication is §-1.1-lethal; refusing is a safe
+    disclosed gap. (Also strictly SAFER than the literal-exact path for the pathological "is not
+    contraindicated" claim, which the OFF path would wrongly credit on the bare substring.)
+    """
+    if any(negation in lowered_claim for negation in _CONTRAINDICATION_NEGATIONS):
+        return False
+    return any(indicator in lowered_claim for indicator in _CONTRAINDICATION_INDICATORS)
+
+

+"""I-perm-002 (#1196) semantic contraindication credit — matcher + behavioral flip.
+
+The CDC contraindication source for the probiotic safety entity says probiotics "should be avoided
+for patients who ... are immunocompromised" — it NEVER uses the word "contraindicated". So a
+FAITHFUL claim grounded in it (drb_76 claim 03-001, "not recommended for patients who are
+immunocompromised", VERIFIED) can never contain the literal token, and the S0 ``contraindications``
+category went un-credited -> ``released_insufficient_safety_evidence`` even though the report DID
+warn. ``PG_SWEEP_SEMANTIC_CONTRAINDICATION`` (default OFF) relaxes ONLY the contraindication CONCEPT
+token to a high-precision DIRECTION synonym set, keeps the population token literal, and refuses
+credit on any negated / opposite-direction phrase.
+
+Direction of error (binding): over-crediting a contraindication is §-1.1-LETHAL; under-crediting is
+a SAFE disclosed gap under always-release (I-perm-001). Every assertion below checks that the
+negation guard makes over-credit impossible while the genuine warning credits.
+"""
+
+from __future__ import annotations
+
+import os
+from pathlib import Path
+
+import pytest
+
+from src.polaris_graph.roles import native_gate_b_inputs as ng
+
+_FLAG = "PG_SWEEP_SEMANTIC_CONTRAINDICATION"
+
+# Inline synthetic entity mirroring config/scope_templates/clinical.yaml
+# `probiotic_immunocompromised_contraindication` — kept inline so the unit tests are robust to
+# config edits. The literal concept token + the literal population anchor.
+_ENTITY = {
+    ng._KEY_ENTITY_ID: "probiotic_immunocompromised_contraindication",
+    ng._KEY_ENTITY_CONTENT_REQS: ["contraindicated", "immunocompromised"],
+}
+
+# The REAL drb_76 claim 03-001 text (VERIFIED, cites the CDC contraindication source). A genuine
+# warning that lacks the literal token "contraindicated".
+_REAL_WARNING = (
+    "On the basis of these data, the authors explicitly advise that probiotics are not "
+    "recommended for patients who are immunocompromised, critically ill, or have indwelling "
+    "catheters."
+)
+
+
+@pytest.fixture(autouse=True)
+def _clear_flag():
+    os.environ.pop(_FLAG, None)
+    yield
+    os.environ.pop(_FLAG, None)
+
+
+# --- OFF: byte-identical literal-exact match (the genuine warning is NOT credited) -----------
+
+
+def test_off_is_literal_exact_unchanged():
+    os.environ.pop(_FLAG, None)
+    # The genuine warning lacks the literal "contraindicated" -> NOT credited (pre-fix behavior).
+    assert ng._content_requirements_satisfied(_REAL_WARNING, _ENTITY) is False
+    # A claim with BOTH literal tokens still credits exactly as before.
+    assert (
+        ng._content_requirements_satisfied(
+            "probiotics are contraindicated in immunocompromised patients", _ENTITY
+        )
+        is True
+    )
+    for falsey in ("0", "false", "no", "off", ""):
+        os.environ[_FLAG] = falsey
+        assert ng._content_requirements_satisfied(_REAL_WARNING, _ENTITY) is False
+
+
+# --- ON: high-precision contraindication-DIRECTION synonyms credit (population stays literal) -
+
+
+@pytest.mark.parametrize(
+    "claim",
+    [
+        _REAL_WARNING,  # "not recommended ... immunocompromised" — the real drb_76 warning
+        "live probiotics should be avoided in immunocompromised hosts",
+        "this product should not be used in immunocompromised patients",
+        "the agent must not be given to immunocompromised individuals",
+        "probiotics are contraindicated in immunocompromised patients",  # literal still works
+    ],
+)
+def test_on_credits_direction_synonyms_with_population(claim):
+    os.environ[_FLAG] = "1"
+    assert ng._content_requirements_satisfied(claim, _ENTITY) is True
+
+
+# --- ON: the negation guard makes over-credit impossible (§-1.1-lethal direction) ------------
+
+
+@pytest.mark.parametrize(
+    "claim",
+    [
+        # Negated contraindication — the pathological case. MUST refuse.
+        "probiotics are not contraindicated in immunocompromised patients",
+        "there is no contraindication to probiotics in immunocompromised hosts",
+        "probiotics should not be avoided in immunocompromised patients",
+        # Opposite direction — safe/recommended for the population. MUST refuse.
+        "probiotics are well tolerated in immunocompromised patients",
+        "probiotics are safe in immunocompromised patients",
+        "probiotics are considered safe for immunocompromised patients",
+    ],
+)
+def test_on_negation_guard_refuses_inverted(claim):
+    os.environ[_FLAG] = "1"
+    assert ng._content_requirements_satisfied(claim, _ENTITY) is False
+
+
+def test_on_is_strictly_safer_than_off_for_negated_contraindicated():
+    """The bare-substring OFF path WRONGLY credits "not contraindicated"; the ON negation guard
+    refuses it. ON is therefore not a pure superset — it ADDS the faithful-warning path and
+    SUBTRACTS this latent over-credit."""
+    inverted = "probiotics are not contraindicated in immunocompromised patients"
+    os.environ.pop(_FLAG, None)
+    assert ng._content_requirements_satisfied(inverted, _ENTITY) is True  # latent OFF over-credit
+    os.environ[_FLAG] = "1"
+    assert ng._content_requirements_satisfied(inverted, _ENTITY) is False  # ON refuses it
+
+
+# --- ON: the population token is NEVER relaxed (wrong population / missing population refuse) --
+
+

+    ],
+)
+def test_on_population_anchor_stays_literal(claim):
+    os.environ[_FLAG] = "1"
+    assert ng._content_requirements_satisfied(claim, _ENTITY) is False
+
+
+def test_on_empty_requirements_fail_closed():
+    os.environ[_FLAG] = "1"
+    empty = {ng._KEY_ENTITY_ID: "x", ng._KEY_ENTITY_CONTENT_REQS: []}
+    assert ng._content_requirements_satisfied(_REAL_WARNING, empty) is False
+    blank = {ng._KEY_ENTITY_ID: "x", ng._KEY_ENTITY_CONTENT_REQS: ["   ", ""]}
+    assert ng._content_requirements_satisfied(_REAL_WARNING, blank) is False
+

+@pytest.mark.skipif(

+def test_drb76_flips_to_released_with_disclosed_gaps():
+    from tests.polaris_graph.replay.d8_replay_harness import (
+        replay_d8,
+        replay_release_outcome,
+    )
+    from tests.polaris_graph.replay.saved_run_loader import (
+        default_run_dir,
+        load_saved_run,

+    os.environ.pop(_FLAG, None)

+    out_off = replay_release_outcome(run, always_release=True, corpus_satisfaction=True)
+    assert out_off.status == "released_insufficient_safety_evidence"
+    assert out_off.safety_floor == "insufficient"

+
+    # ON: the contraindication category credits from the faithful warning -> safety floor clears ->
+    # caveated-normal release. The credit comes from a VERIFIED claim that genuinely warned; nothing
+    # is fabricated, and coverage rises by exactly the one contraindication element.
+    os.environ[_FLAG] = "1"
+    d_on = replay_d8(run, corpus_satisfaction=True)

+    assert out_on.safety_floor == "ok"
+    assert out_on.normal_release_blocked is False

  293: def _entity_canonical_match(entity: dict, record: Mapping[str, Any]) -> bool:
  294:     """EXACT canonical-identifier match (P2 #4): DOI / PMID / full canonical URL.
  295: 
  296:     Equality per (entity_key, record_key) pair only — never substring. The URL pair compares
  297:     the entity's `url_pattern` against the record's resolved `url`; a broad `url_pattern`
  298:     FRAGMENT can never equal a full resolved URL, so it fails closed.
  299:     """
  300:     for entity_key, record_key in _CANONICAL_MATCH_PAIRS:
  301:         entity_value = entity.get(entity_key)
  302:         if entity_value in (None, ""):
  303:             continue
  304:         record_value = record.get(record_key)
  305:         if record_value in (None, ""):
  306:             continue
  307:         if str(entity_value).strip() == str(record_value).strip():
  308:             return True
  309:     return False
  310:

  312: # --- I-perm-002 (#1196): semantic contraindication credit (default OFF -> literal-exact) -----
  313: # The CDC contraindication source for `probiotic_immunocompromised_contraindication` says probiotics
  314: # "should be avoided for patients who ... are immunocompromised" — it NEVER uses the word
  315: # "contraindicated". So a FAITHFUL claim grounded in that span (e.g. drb_76 claim 03-001, "not
  316: # recommended for patients who are immunocompromised", VERIFIED + cites the exact CDC url) can never
  317: # contain the literal token, and the S0 `contraindications` category goes un-credited -> the safety
  318: # floor reports insufficient even though the report DID warn. PG_SWEEP_SEMANTIC_CONTRAINDICATION
  319: # relaxes ONLY the contraindication-CONCEPT requirement token to a curated, high-precision set of
  320: # contraindication-DIRECTION phrases; the population token (e.g. "immunocompromised") is NEVER
  321: # relaxed — it stays the literal precision anchor that binds substance+population (R6). A
  322: # deterministic negation guard refuses credit on any opposite-direction / negated phrase so a
  323: # faithful-but-INVERTED claim can never earn S0 safety credit.
  324: #
  325: # Direction of error is deliberate: over-crediting a contraindication is §-1.1-LETHAL (a report that
  326: # wrongly believes it warned); UNDER-crediting is a SAFE disclosed gap under always-release
  327: # (I-perm-001). Every layer therefore errs toward refusing credit. Four independent guards stand
  328: # between a claim and S0 credit: (1) `verification.is_verified` — only strict-verified claims reach
  329: # the matcher; (2) `_entity_canonical_match` — the claim must cite the entity's EXACT source;
  330: # (3) the literal population anchor; (4) this negation guard.
  331: _ENV_SEMANTIC_CONTRAINDICATION = "PG_SWEEP_SEMANTIC_CONTRAINDICATION"
  332: _SEMANTIC_OFF_VALUES = frozenset({"", "0", "false", "no", "off"})
  333: # Requirement tokens that NAME the contraindication concept — only these are relaxed to synonyms.
  334: _CONTRAINDICATION_CONCEPT_TOKENS = frozenset({"contraindicated", "contraindication"})
  335: # High-precision contraindication-DIRECTION surface forms. Deliberately MULTI-WORD (bare "avoid" /
  336: # "unsafe" / "risk" are too ambiguous and would over-credit); each phrase unambiguously asserts
  337: # "do not give this to this population". Lowercased; substring-tested against the lowered claim.
  338: _CONTRAINDICATION_INDICATORS = (
  339:     "contraindicated",
  340:     "contraindication",
  341:     "not recommended",
  342:     "should be avoided",
  343:     "should not be used",
  344:     "should not be given",
  345:     "should not be administered",
  346:     "must not be used",
  347:     "must not be given",
  348:     "must not be administered",
  349:     "not be used in",
  350: )
  351: # Opposite-direction / negated-contraindication phrases. If ANY appears ANYWHERE in the claim, refuse
  352: # credit (whole-claim scan, fail-closed): a faithful-but-inverted statement must NEVER satisfy an S0
  353: # contraindication category. Over-broad here only causes a SAFE under-credit (disclosed gap), so the
  354: # list errs wide. NOTE the contiguity asymmetry that makes the positive/negative sets coexist: "are
  355: # not recommended" contains the positive "not recommended" but NOT the contiguous negative
  356: # "are recommended"; "is not contraindicated" contains the negative "not contraindicated" so it is
  357: # correctly refused even though "contraindicated" is a positive indicator substring.
  358: _CONTRAINDICATION_NEGATIONS = (
  359:     "not contraindicated",
  360:     "no contraindication",
  361:     "not a contraindication",
  362:     "without contraindication",
  363:     "should not be avoided",
  364:     "need not be avoided",
  365:     "not be avoided",
  366:     "well tolerated",
  367:     "well-tolerated",
  368:     "is recommended",
  369:     "are recommended",
  370:     "is safe",
  371:     "are safe",
  372:     "considered safe",
  373:     "generally safe",
  374:     "safely used",
  375:     "safely administered",
  376: )
  377: 
  378: 
  379: def _semantic_contraindication_enabled() -> bool:
  380:     """``PG_SWEEP_SEMANTIC_CONTRAINDICATION`` (default OFF -> literal-exact content match)."""
  381:     return os.environ.get(_ENV_SEMANTIC_CONTRAINDICATION, "").strip().lower() not in _SEMANTIC_OFF_VALUES
  382: 
  383: 
  384: def _contraindication_direction_present(lowered_claim: str) -> bool:
  385:     """True iff the claim asserts a contraindication DIRECTION and does NOT negate it.
  386: 
  387:     Fail-closed: a negated / opposite-direction phrase refuses credit even when a positive
  388:     indicator co-occurs — over-crediting a contraindication is §-1.1-lethal; refusing is a safe
  389:     disclosed gap. (Also strictly SAFER than the literal-exact path for the pathological "is not
  390:     contraindicated" claim, which the OFF path would wrongly credit on the bare substring.)
  391:     """
  392:     if any(negation in lowered_claim for negation in _CONTRAINDICATION_NEGATIONS):
  393:         return False
  394:     return any(indicator in lowered_claim for indicator in _CONTRAINDICATION_INDICATORS)
  395: 
  396: 
  397: def _content_requirements_satisfied(claim_text: str, entity: dict) -> bool:
  398:     """All `coverage_content_requirements` present in the claim (case-insensitive, exact).
  399: 
  400:     FAIL CLOSED (Codex P1): if the requirements list is empty — or somehow all-blank — return
  401:     False rather than vacuously True. A bare canonical-evidence citation must NEVER earn S0
  402:     credit without a real deterministic content match. (Validation in `validate_entity_severity`
  403:     already blocks empty/blank S0 requirements at load; this is the matcher-side backstop.)
  404: 
  405:     I-perm-002 (#1196): when `PG_SWEEP_SEMANTIC_CONTRAINDICATION` is ON, a requirement token that
  406:     NAMES the contraindication concept ("contraindicated"/"contraindication") is satisfied by any
  407:     high-precision contraindication-DIRECTION phrase (negation-guarded); every OTHER token (e.g.
  408:     "immunocompromised") stays literal-exact. Default OFF -> byte-identical literal-exact match.
  409:     """
  410:     lowered = claim_text.lower()
  411:     requirements = [
  412:         token
  413:         for token in entity.get(_KEY_ENTITY_CONTENT_REQS) or []
  414:         if isinstance(token, str) and token.strip()
  415:     ]
  416:     if not requirements:
  417:         return False
  418:     semantic = _semantic_contraindication_enabled()
  419:     for token in requirements:
  420:         token_lower = token.lower()
  421:         if semantic and token_lower in _CONTRAINDICATION_CONCEPT_TOKENS:
  422:             if not _contraindication_direction_present(lowered):
  423:                 return False
  424:         elif token_lower not in lowered:
  425:             return False
  426:     return True
  427: 
  428: 
  429: def _claim_covers_entity(
  430:     claim_evidence_records: list[Mapping[str, Any]],
  431:     claim_text: str,
  432:     entity: dict,
  433:     *,
  434:     is_s0: bool,
  435: ) -> bool:
  436:     """Coverage test (§7.D, Codex-tightened + P2 #4).
  437: 
  438:     ENTITY coverage: True iff the claim cites evidence whose CANONICAL identifier EXACTLY
  439:     matches the entity's. anchor-string-in-sentence alone does NOT grant coverage.
  440: 
  441:     S0 SAFETY-CATEGORY coverage (stricter): an S0 entity is covered ONLY when BOTH (a) the
  442:     canonical evidence match holds AND (b) the claim text deterministically satisfies the
  443:     entity's `coverage_content_requirements`. A broad source citation without the content
  444:     match does NOT satisfy an S0 safety category.
  445:     """
  446:     if not any(_entity_canonical_match(entity, rec) for rec in claim_evidence_records):
  447:         return False
  448:     if is_s0 and not _content_requirements_satisfied(claim_text, entity):
  449:         return False
  450:     return True
  451:

  453: # FX-03 / I-ready-017 (#1107): the 4-role seam (Sentinel decomposition + Judge) joins each

  455: # was passed, so a claim could be graded VERIFIED on support living ANYWHERE in the document, not
  456: # in the cited [start:end] window (BUG-02: confirmed out-of-span false-accept, claim 06-004).
  457: _GATE_B_CITED_SPAN_ENV = "PG_GATE_B_CITED_SPAN"
  458: _GATE_B_SPAN_WINDOW_ENV = "PG_GATE_B_SPAN_WINDOW_BYTES"
  459: _GATE_B_SPAN_WINDOW_DEFAULT = 400
  460: 
  461: 
  462: def _cited_window_text(full_text: str, token: Any) -> str:
  463:     """Return the cited BOUNDED-WINDOW slice of ``full_text`` for one provenance token.
  464: 
  465:     When ``PG_GATE_B_CITED_SPAN=1`` the evidence text handed to Sentinel/Judge is sliced to a
  466:     bounded window around the cited ``[start:end]`` range (±``PG_GATE_B_SPAN_WINDOW_BYTES``, default
  467:     400) — the SAME tolerance strict_verify's local-window rescue uses, so the two faithfulness
  468:     layers share ONE windowing policy. BOUNDED (not exact-slice) is deliberate and three-fold safer:
  469:     (1) it tolerates the 06-004-shape imprecise-but-real citation that strict_verify also tolerates;
  470:     (2) ``token.start/end`` index the RAW ``direct_quote`` while the record text here was

   78: def _load_required_entities(template_path: Path, slug: str) -> _RequiredEntities:
   79:     """Reuse the production helpers so the required-set + S0 derivation match the real builder."""
   80:     template = yaml.safe_load(template_path.read_text(encoding="utf-8"))
   81:     d8_config = load_d8_policy_config()
   82:     entities = load_required_entities(template, slug)

   89:         s0_by_element[entity[_KEY_ENTITY_ID]] = s0_category
   90:         if s0_category is not None:
   91:             required_s0.add(s0_category)
   92:     return _RequiredEntities(element_ids, sorted(required_s0), s0_by_element, entity_by_id)

   95: def verified_claims_citing(run: SavedRun, evidence_id: str) -> list[str]:

  106: _MISSING_S0_PREFIX = "d8_s0_must_cover_missing:"

  108: 
  109: def replay_release_outcome(
  110:     run: SavedRun,
  111:     *,
  112:     always_release: bool,
  113:     template_path: Path | None = None,
  114:     corpus_satisfaction: bool = False,
  115: ) -> ReleaseOutcome:
  116:     """Replay the I-perm-001 always-release outcome (BLOCK -> LABEL) over a saved run.
  117: 
  118:     Computes the no-fabrication hard line + the clinical safety floor from the saved verdicts:
  119:     ``zero_verified`` (no VERIFIED claim), ``zero_usable_evidence`` (no cited evidence at all),
  120:     ``safety_floor_insufficient`` (every required S0 SAFETY category is in the missing set).
  121: 
  122:     ``corpus_satisfaction`` (I-perm-002 #1196): when True, the safety floor is computed from
  123:     coverage RE-DERIVED through the production S0 content-requirement matcher
  124:     (``_content_requirements_satisfied``) rather than the saved literal-era ``covered_element_ids``.
  125:     With ``PG_SWEEP_SEMANTIC_CONTRAINDICATION`` ON this credits a faithful contraindication warning
  126:     ("not recommended"/"should be avoided") that the literal-token era left un-credited — so the
  127:     safety floor clears and a run that was ``released_insufficient_safety_evidence`` becomes a
  128:     caveated ``released_with_disclosed_gaps``. Default False preserves the I-perm-009/I-perm-001
  129:     baseline (literal-era coverage, bit-for-bit).
  130:     """
  131:     result = replay_d8(
  132:         run, template_path=template_path, corpus_satisfaction=corpus_satisfaction
  133:     )
  134:     decision = result.decision
  135:     zero_verified = not any(v == _VERDICT_VERIFIED for v in run.final_verdicts.values())
  136:     zero_usable_evidence = not any(
  137:         run.audit_map.get(cid, {}).get("evidence_ids") for cid in run.final_verdicts
  138:     )
  139:     missing_s0 = {
  140:         reason[len(_MISSING_S0_PREFIX):]
  141:         for reason in decision.held_reasons
  142:         if reason.startswith(_MISSING_S0_PREFIX)
  143:     }
  144:     required_s0 = set(result.required_s0_categories)
  145:     safety_floor_insufficient = bool(required_s0) and required_s0 <= missing_s0
  146:     return compute_release_outcome(
  147:         decision,
  148:         zero_verified=zero_verified,
  149:         zero_usable_evidence=zero_usable_evidence,
  150:         safety_floor_insufficient=safety_floor_insufficient,
  151:         coverage_fraction=result.coverage_fraction,
  152:         always_release=always_release,
  153:     )
  154: 
  155: 
  156: def replay_d8(
  157:     run: SavedRun,
  158:     *,
  159:     template_path: Path | None = None,
  160:     corpus_satisfaction: bool = False,
  161:     rewrite_already_attempted: bool = False,
  162: ) -> D8ReplayResult:
  163:     """Replay the production D8 release policy over a saved run's recorded verdicts."""
  164:     tpath = template_path or default_template_path()
  165:     req = _load_required_entities(tpath, run.slug)
  166:     required_set = set(req.element_ids)
  167: 
  168:     credited_categories: set[str] = set()
  169:     d8_rows: list[D8ClaimRow] = []
  170:     covered: set[str] = set()
  171: 
  172:     for claim_id, verdict in run.final_verdicts.items():
  173:         audit_row = run.audit_map[claim_id]
  174:         claim_text = str(audit_row.get("sentence", ""))
  175:         s0_categories = list(audit_row.get("s0_categories", []))
  176: 
  177:         if corpus_satisfaction and verdict == _VERDICT_VERIFIED:
  178:             for evidence_id in audit_row.get("evidence_ids", []):
  179:                 if evidence_id not in required_set:
  180:                     continue
  181:                 category = req.s0_by_element.get(evidence_id)
  182:                 if category is None:
  183:                     # Non-S0 required element: canonical (evidence_id) match credits coverage,
  184:                     # mirroring production _claim_covers_entity for a non-S0 entity.
  185:                     covered.add(evidence_id)
  186:                     continue
  187:                 # S0 SAFETY: credit ONLY when the claim text satisfies the deterministic
  188:                 # content requirements (faithful to production; no evidence_id-alone credit).
  189:                 entity = req.entity_by_id[evidence_id]
  190:                 if _content_requirements_satisfied(claim_text, entity):
  191:                     covered.add(evidence_id)
  192:                     if category not in s0_categories:
  193:                         s0_categories.append(category)
  194:                     credited_categories.add(category)
  195: 
  196:         if verdict == _VERDICT_VERIFIED:
  197:             covered.update(audit_row.get("covered_element_ids", []))
  198: 
  199:         d8_rows.append(
  200:             D8ClaimRow(
  201:                 claim_id=claim_id,
  202:                 severity=audit_row["severity"],
  203:                 verdict=verdict,
  204:                 s0_categories=s0_categories,
  205:             )
  206:         )
  207: 
  208:     covered &= required_set
  209:     ledger = CoverageLedger(required_element_ids=req.element_ids, covered_element_ids=covered)
  210:     d8_config = load_d8_policy_config()
  211:     decision = apply_d8_release_policy(
  212:         d8_rows,
  213:         required_s0_categories=req.s0_categories,
  214:         coverage_ledger=ledger,
  215:         coverage_threshold=d8_config.coverage_threshold,
  216:         rewrite_already_attempted=rewrite_already_attempted,
  217:         prior_fabricated_latched=run.saved_fabricated_latched,
  218:     )
  219:     return D8ReplayResult(
  220:         decision=decision,
  221:         coverage_fraction=ledger.fraction(),
  222:         required_element_ids=req.element_ids,
  223:         required_s0_categories=req.s0_categories,
  224:         covered_element_ids=covered,
  225:         credited_categories=credited_categories,
  226:     )

Rannikko); Tampere University Hospital, Tampere (J. Rannikko, M. Karppelin, P. Arvola, R. Huttunen, J. Syrj\u00e4nen); University of Helsinki, Helsinki, Finland (V. Tamm, I. Koivula); Fimlab Laboratories, Tampere (R. Vuento); Turku University Hospital, Turku, Finland (U. Hohenthal)\n\n[Cite This Article](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article# \"Show Article Citations\")\n\n[Citation for Media](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/press)\n\n### Introduction\n\n![Image 1: CME Logo](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/Content/images/CME-JAP-logo.jpg?v=1.1)\n\nMedscape CME ACTIVITY\n\nIn support of improving patient care, this activity has been planned and implemented by Medscape, LLC and Emerging Infectious Diseases. Medscape, LLC is jointly accredited by the Accreditation Council for Continuing Medical Education (ACCME), the Accreditation Council for Pharmacy Education (ACPE), and the American Nurses Credentialing Center (ANCC), to provide continuing education for the healthcare team.\n\nMedscape, LLC designates this Journal-based CME activity for a maximum of 1.00 **_AMA PRA Category 1 Credit(s)_\u2122**. Physicians should claim only the credit commensurate with the extent of their participation in the activity.\n\nSuccessful completion of this CME activity, which includes participation in the evaluation component, enables the participant to earn up to 1.0 MOC points in the American Board of Internal Medicine's (ABIM) Maintenance of Certification (MOC) program. Participants will earn MOC points equivalent to the amount of CME credits claimed for the activity. It is the CME activity provider's responsibility to submit participant completion information to ACCME for the purpose of granting ABIM MOC credit.\n\nAll other clinicians completing this activity will be issued a certificate of participation. To participate in this journal CME activity: (1) review the learning objectives and author disclosures; (2) study the education content; (3) take the post-test with a 75% minimum passing score and complete the evaluation at [https://www.medscape.org/journal/eid](https://www.medscape.org/journal/eid); and (4) view/print certificate.\n\nRelease date: July 14, 2021; Expiration date: July 14, 2022\n\nLearning Objectives\n\nUpon completion of this activity, participants will be able to:\n\n*   Describe the use and clinical characteristics of _Saccharomyces boulardii_ (_Sb_) probiotic yeast among patients with _Saccharomyces_ fungemia, according to a retrospective registry study of all _Saccharomyces_ sp. findings during 2009\u20132018 in 5 university hospitals in Finland\n\n*   Determine the use of _Sb_ probiotic yeast among patients with positive _Saccharomyces_ culture findings in samples other than blood, according to a retrospective registry study of all _Saccharomyces_ sp. findings during 2009\u20132018 in 5 university hospitals in Finland\n\n*   Identify clinical implications of the use of _Sb_ probiotic yeast among patients with _Saccharomyces_ fungemia or other positive _Saccharomyces_ culture findings, according to a retrospective registry study of all _Saccharomyces_ sp. findings during 2009\u20132018 in 5 university hospitals in Finland\n\nCME Editor\n\n**Thomas J. Gryczan, MS,** Technical Writer/Editor, Emerging Infectious Diseases. _Disclosure: Thomas J. Gryczan, MS, has disclosed no relevant financial relationships._\n\nCME Author\n\n**Laurie Barclay, MD,** freelance writer and reviewer, Medscape, LLC. _Disclosure: Laurie Barclay, MD, has disclosed no relevant financial relationships._\n\nAuthors\n\n_Disclosures:_**_Juha Rannikko, MD, PhD; Ville Holmberg, MD, PhD; Matti Karppelin, MD, PhD; Pertti Arvola, MD, PhD; Reetta Huttunen, MD, PhD; Eero Mattila, MD, PhD; Niina Kerttula, MD, MS; Teija Puhto, MD, PhD; \u00dclle Tamm, MD; Irma Koivula, MD, PhD; Risto Vuento, MD, PhD;_**_and_**_Jaana Syrj\u00e4nen, MD, PhD,_**_have disclosed no relevant financial relationships._**_Ulla Hohenthal, MD, PhD,_**_has disclosed the following relevant financial relationships: served as an advisor or consultant for GlaxoSmithKline; other (Congress Travel) for Grifols Nordic AB; Merck Sharp & Dohme GmbH._\n\nTop\n\n### Abstract\n\nBecause of widespread use of probiotics, their safety must be guaranteed. We assessed use of _Saccharomyces boulardii_ probiotic yeast from medical records for patients who had _Saccharomyces_ fungemia or other clinical _Saccharomyces_ culture findings. We evaluated all _Saccharomyces_ sp. findings at 5 university hospitals in Finland during 2009\u20132018. We found 46 patients who had _Saccharomyces_ fungemia; at least 20 (43%) were using _S. boulardii_ probiotic. Compared with a control group that had bacteremia or candidemia, the odds ratio for use of an _S. boulardii_ probiotic was 14 (95% CI 4\u201344). Of 1,153 nonblood culture findings, the history for 125 patients was checked; at least 24 (19%) were using the probiotic (odds ratio 10, 95% CI 3\u201332). This study adds to published fungemia cases linked to use of _S. boulardii_ probiotic and sheds light on the scale of nonblood _Saccharomyces_ culture findings that are also linked to use of this probiotic.\n\nProbiotics are live microorganisms intended to provide health benefits when consumed ([_1_](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article#r21 \"1\")). Typically, the endpoint in randomized controlled trials of probiotics has been the prevention of diarrhea or faster alleviation of diarrhea symptoms ([_2_](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article#r22 \"2\")). Regarding their safety, serious adverse effects have been rare in probiotic studies ([_3_](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article#r23 \"3\")). However, the adverse effects have not been fully reported ([_4_](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article#r24 \"4\")). In 1 trial in which a multispecies probiotic preparation was given to patients who had severe acute pancreatitis, the mortality rate was higher in the probiotic arm ([_5_](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article#r25 \"5\")). Nevertheless, the use of probiotics is common. According to the 2012 National Health Interview Survey in the United States, 1.6% of adults had used prebiotics or probiotics in the preceding 30 days ([_6_](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article#r26 \"6\")).\n\n_Saccharomyces cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ is a yeast that is used as a probiotic. In hospitals in the United States, the use of _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ has been common, especially among elderly patients ([_7_](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article#r27 \"7\")). This strain is difficult to distinguish microbiologically from _S. cerevisiae_ because they have >99% genomic relatedness ([_8_](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article#r28 \"8\")). Thus, in everyday clinical laboratory work, the _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ strain is identified as either _Saccharomyces_ sp _._ or _S. cerevisiae_. A review from 2005 considered _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ to be the etiologic agent of _Saccharomyces_ fungemia if the patient received treatment with a probiotic containing _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ or if a molecular typing method confirmed the identification of this yeast ([_9_](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article#r29 \"9\")). The authors found 37 cases in the literature. We found an additional 14 reports, including 22 cases of _Saccharomyces_ fungemia with the same diagnostic method published after this review ([_10_](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article#r210 \"10\")_\u2013_[_23_](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article#r223 \"23\")). Thus, before our study, 59 cases of fungemia with a link to the use of the probiotic had been published. All of these cases have been either individual cases or small cases series (<7 cases) without any systematic approach to quantify the problem.\n\nFurthermore, besides fungemia, there are few reports on other clinically relevant microbiological findings for this yeast (i.e., in abscesses, ascites fluid, or the pleural cavity). The meta-analysis we mention listed 20 cases of findings other than fungemia ([_9_](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article#r29 \"9\")). These findings are useful because they might also lead to a change in antimicrobial treatment.\n\nWe conducted a retrospective registry study (case series) at 5 university hospitals in Finland to evaluate the use of the _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ probiotic in patients who had _Saccharomyces_ fungemia or another clinical culture finding for this yeast. To evaluate the association between probiotic use and subsequent findings, we compared use of _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ for patients who had a _Saccharomyces_ infection with use of _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ for patients who had an infection caused by another etiologic agent, such as bacteria or _Candida_ sp.\n\n##### Background\n\nFinland has 5 university hospitals that are secondary referral centers of their catchment areas and tertiary referral centers for other hospital districts. Their combined catchment areas cover more than half population of Finland (3.29 million of 5.6 million persons). All university hospitals use the same register (SAI-registry; Neotide Ltd, [https://www.neotide.fi](https://www.neotide.fi/)), in which the local clinical microbial laboratory data are collected. These data cover all blood culture data and most of all other clinical microbial culture data of the catchment area of the university hospital.\n\n##### Patient Data\n\nAt least 1 infectious diseases specialist in every university hospital collected the clinical data from the medical records for all blood culture-positive cases found in the register that were identified as _Saccharomyces_ sp. or _S. cerevisiae_. The use of the _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ probiotic was defined as use at the time of the positive culture or in the preceding 7 days. Data were collected on use during the preceding 3 months. If the medical record was not available, the case-patient was classified as not using a probiotic. The Quick SOFA score and the definition of septic shock were based on the Sepsis\u20103 definitions ([_24_](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article#r224 \"24\")). The McCabe score was determined as reported by McCabe and Jackson ([_25_](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article#r225 \"25\")). Data collected for case-patients who had nonblood cultures were age, sex, malignancy, digestive tract disease, use of probiotics, use of antifungal medication at the time of the culture, and possible change of medication resulting from finding of _Saccharomyces_ sp. The most recent 25 cases of nonblood culture findings in each hospital district were evaluated (excluding case-patients who had positive fecal samples). Isolates were obtained from routine laboratory bacterial and fungal cultures. The anatomic site of the culture was collected from the local hospital microbial registry (SAI) for all culture-positive cases. Abdominal sites were those in which culture was taken from, for example, ascites fluid, a biliary drainage catheter, or abscess drainage fluid, but not from skin or wound secretions. Oral and respiratory tract samples were from sinus drainage, bronchial lavage cultures, and pleural drainage. Other sites included samples from perianal abscesses, mediastinum, and urine.\n\n##### Control Group\n\nTo evaluate the practice of probiotic use in the hospital ward in which the patient who had a _Saccharomyces_ finding was given treatment, a control group was obtained from the same SAI register. For every _Saccharomyces_ fungemia case-patient, 2 blood culture-positive patients (1 chronologically closest before and 1 after) from the same ward as the case were selected. For every clinical culture sample (other than blood), there was 1 chronologically closest positive culture sample from the same ward as the case-patient who served as a control. Data collected for the controls were date, ward, microbe, age, sex, malignancy, digestive tract disease, and _S. cerevisiae var. boulardii_ probiotic use at the time of the positive culture or in the previous 3 months.\n\n##### Statistical Analysis\n\nWe used SPSS version 22.0 software (IBM Corp., [https://www.ibm.com](https://www.ibm.com/)) for statistical analyses. The study was centrally approved by the Ethics Committee of Tampere University Hospital, Tampere, Finland. The requirement for informed consent was waived.\n\n##### Blood Cultures\n\nThere were 46 patients with a positive blood culture for _Saccharomyces_ in the 5 hospitals during between January 2009\u2012December 2018. The median age of case-patients was 68 (range 30\u201393) years and a male predominance (63%). The most common underlying condition was a digestive tract disease (59%). There was a medical record confirming the use of the _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ probiotic on the day of the blood culture or during the preceding 7 days for 20 case-patients (43%). Medical records were not available for 10 case-patients (22%), and these were classified as nonusers.\n\nOf the 20 case-patients, 17 were using _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ probiotic on the day of the blood culture and 3 case-patients had already stopped using it (2 patients on the day before and 1 patient 5 days earlier). Five additional case-patients had used the probiotic in the preceding 3 months, of whom 1 patient had already stopped using it 26 days earlier. For 4 case-patients, the time when the use of the probiotic was terminated could not be determined. Most case-patients (16/20, 80%) received the _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ probiotic in the hospital, 3 case-patients in some other facility, and 1 case-patient was using it at home. All _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ probiotics found in the medical records were from the same strain (Precosa; Biocodex Ltd., [https://www.biocodex.com](https://www.biocodex.com/)). We provide characteristics, underlying diseases, and severity of the disease for these patients ([Tables 1](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/T21),[2](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/T22)).\n\nAntimicrobial drugs were commonly used by the patients (72%) during 4 weeks preceding the fungemia. Antifungal treatment was commenced or changed because of _Saccharomyces_ fungemia for 23 patients (50%). For an additional 8 patients (17%), the culture result came after the patient had died. Case-fatality rates by day 7 were 22% (10 patients) and by day 28 were 37% (17 patients). Of patients who died by day 28, 6 patients had an ultimately fatal disease (McCabe score 2) and 5 patients had a rapidly fatal disease (McCabe score 3).\n\n##### Nonblood Cultures\n\nThere were 1,153 nonblood _Saccharomyces_ culture findings (fecal samples excluded). There was considerable variation between hospital districts in numbers of the microbial cultures and anatomic sites from which cultures were obtained ([Table 3](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/T23)). We evaluated use of probiotics for 125 case-patients. Medical records were not available for 6 of them. Use of _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ probiotic was confirmed for 24 case-patients (19%). This finding was divided by the anatomic site as follows: 17 (21%) of 82 from the abdominal region, 4 (13%) of 30 from the oral or respiratory tract, and 3 (23%) of 13 from other sites. Antifungal medication was already in use at the time of culture for 38% (47/125, the medical record was not available for 1 case-patient) of the case-patients. This finding led to a modification of the antifungal medication in for 35% (44/125, medical records not available for 2 case-patients) of the case-patients.\n\n##### Controls\n\nThe controls for the fungemia case-patients (n = 76) were mostly bacteremic (n = 65), but there were 5 case-patients infected with _Candida_ sp. Medical records were not available for 6 control case-patients. Median age for this group was 70 years (vs. 68 years for case-patients), 70% were males (versus 63% for the case-patients), 47% had digestive tract disease (vs. 59% of the case-patients), and 17% had a malignancy (vs. 13% of the case-patients) (data were available for 64 case-patients). Use of _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ probiotic by the _Saccharomyces_ fungemia group was 43% compared with 5% (4/76) for the control group (odds ratio [OR] 14, 95% CI 4\u201344).\n\nMicrobes detected for controls who had nonblood cultures (n = 123) were also mostly bacteria (n = 97), but _Candida_ sp. or other yeasts (n = 51; with or without a concomitant bacterial finding) were more common than in blood cultures. Median age for this group was 65 years (vs. 64 years for case-patients), 44% were males (vs. 59% for case-patients), 70% had digestive tract disease (vs. 69% of case-patients), and 28% had a malignancy (vs. 38% of case-patients) (data on underlying diseases were available for 100 controls). Use of _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ probiotic was 19% (24/125) in the _Saccharomyces_ culture-positive group compared with 2% (3/123) for the control group (OR 10, 95% CI 3\u201332).\n\nWe report 20 cases of _Saccharomyces_ fungemia in patients who used _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ probiotic. These cases have increased the number of cases reported in the literature by 34%.\n\nWe evaluated 125 of 1,153 patients who had a nonblood _Saccharomyces_ culture finding and confirmed use of _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ probiotic by 19% of these case-patients. To our knowledge, the magnitude of findings other than fungemia has not been reported. Although some of these nonblood findings might represent colonization, positive _Saccharomyces_ cultures led to a change in antimicrobial drugs for 44 (35%) patients who had evaluated cases.\n\nWe also evaluated use of _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ probiotic for patients who had a _Saccharomyces_ culture finding and compared it with that of a control group who had different positive blood and nonblood cultures and were in the same ward around the same time. The _Saccharomyces_ fungemia patients had an OR of 14 and nonblood culture-positive patients had an OR of 10 for use of this probiotic compared with respective controls. Moreover, case-patient 7 ([Table 1](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/T21)) is an example of a patient in whom probiotic use unequivocally caused the fungemia. The patient had had a percutaneous endoscopic gastrostomy feeding tube inserted 2 days before the fungemia, had septic shock, and then died. The probiotic was administered at least once through the tube, and the tip of the tube was unintentionally displaced from its ventricular position, leading to an accidental peritoneal application of the probiotic.\n\n_Saccharomyces_ fungemias occurred most often in patients who had diseases of the gastrointestinal tract (59%). This finding is nearly identical to the amount reported in a meta-analysis (58%) ([_9_](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article#r29 \"9\")). Furthermore, there are reports of _Saccharomyces_ fungemias in patients not given pretreatment with a _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ probiotic that have been believed to have been derived from contaminated central venous catheters ([_26_](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article#r226 \"26\")_\u2013_[_28_](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article#r228 \"28\")).\n\nBacteremias and fungemias have not been encountered in clinical trials with probiotics in general. There were probiotic studies conducted with susceptible patients who did not have blood culture findings and who had hepatic encephalopathy ([_29_](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article#r229 \"29\")). However, serious concurrent conditions have usually been an exclusion criterion; thus, the safety profile remains unclear. Furthermore, there are other reported safety issues with probiotics, such as contamination of a probiotic supplement with a pathogenic microbe or possible transfer of an antimicrobial drug resistance gene from a probiotic microbe to pathogenic microbes ([_30_](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article#r230 \"30\")_\u2013_[_32_](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article#r232 \"32\")).\n\nRegarding the benefits of probiotics, is there evidence showing that adults should use _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ probiotic in conjunction with antimicrobial drugs to prevent _Clostridioides difficile_ infections (CDIs) that cause diarrhea? A meta-analysis during 2017 combined _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ probiotic studies conducted in adult populations to prevent CDIs ([_33_](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article#r233 \"33\")). There were 5 studies. All studies had a low number of CDIs (15 cases of CDI in control groups) and all had nonsigficant results (pooled risk ratio 0.63, 95% CI 0.29\u20131.37).\n\nCurrently, several companies sell _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ yeast, but total consumption of this yeast in Finland is not known. Thus, we are not able to relate our study results to its use. However, nationwide consumption of probiotics does not reflect the risk for fungemia or nonblood culture findings, or use of this probiotic by susceptible patients in hospitals. Cautious use of _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ probiotic in gastrointestinal surgery wards would probably be one of the most effective ways to decrease these culture findings.\n\nMoreover, the benefits for the indication for which the probiotic is used need to be established. There are 2 recent US guidelines on administration of probiotics in general for primary prevention of CDI. The first guideline states that there are insufficient data to recommend the administration ([_34_](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article#r234 \"34\")), and the second guideline states that in certain circumstances certain probiotics could be used, but the quality of evidence is low ([_35_](https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/27/8/21-0018_article#r235 \"35\")).\n\nThe first limitation of this study is that we were not able to obtain microbiological evidence that the fungal infections were caused by the _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ probiotic strain and not by another strain. Furthermore, the retrospective design using medical records can lead to a bias in reporting. Only confirmed use of probiotics was reported in this study, and case-patients whose medical records were not available were defined as not using a probiotic. Thus, the percentage of probiotic users was the minimum estimate in all groups. All medications given to patients in the wards were documented in medical records of patients, but patients might have used these medications before they were admitted to the hospital. Moreover, most patients who had fungemia were given bacterial antimicrobial drugs, which could have decreased the routine of taking blood cultures. Recall bias (e.g., the _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ probiotic would have been mentioned in the medical charts more often in case-patients than in control-patients because of _Saccharomyces_ culture finding) was not a limitation in this study. For all but 1 case-patient, the probiotic was recorded in the charts before the culture result was complete.\n\n_S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ probiotics are not recommended for patients who have indwelling catheters, are immunocompromised, or are critically ill. Our results indicate that use of _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ probiotics should also be considered carefully for patients whose gastrointestinal tract integrity might be compromised. Furthermore, more data are needed to elucidate the health benefits of _S. cerevisiae_ var. _boulardii_ probiotics in general .\n\nTop\n\n### Acknowledgment\n\nThis study was supported by scholarships from the Finnish Medical Foundation (grant 2903 to J.R.) and Competitive State Research Financing of the Expert Responsibility Area of Tampere University Hospital (grant 92010 to R.H.).\n\nTop\n\n### References\n\n1. The effectiveness of probiotics can be enhanced by formulating them with prebiotics the formulation thus formed is referred to as synbiotics. It not only improves the viability and stability of probiotic cells, but also inhibits the growth of pathogenic strains. Lactobacillus and Bifidobacterium spp. are most commonly used as probiotics. The other microbial spp. that can be used as probiotics are Bacillus, Streptococcus, Enterococcus, and Saccharomyces. Probiotics can be used for the treatment of diabetes, obesity, inflammatory, cardiovascular, respiratory, Central nervous system disease (CNS) and digestive disorders. It is also essential to encapsulate live microorganisms that promote intestinal health. Encapsulation of probiotics safeguards them against risks during production, storage, and gastrointestinal transit. Heat, pressure, and oxidation eradicate probiotics and their protective qualities. Encapsulation of probiotics prolongs their viability, facilitates regulated release, reduces processing losses, and enables application in functional food products. Do NOT use generic plural language like "both agencies", "all regulators", "authorities generally", "regulators require", "jurisdictions mandate", or similar when the evidence you cite comes from a single jurisdiction. A boxed warning in one jurisdiction is not the same legal instrument as a precaution in another jurisdiction; a formal contraindication in one framework is not automatically equivalent to a warning in another. If the evidence supports only one jurisdiction's position, write: "Jurisdiction A's framework classifies X as a contraindication [ev_A]. Jurisdiction B's framework addresses X through warnings and precautions [ev_B]." Only collapse to "both" / "all" / "generally" when you have a citation from each referenced jurisdiction in the SAME sentence proving the shared position.

citations are the body's._\n\n- **Efficacy.** Population: patients with advanced Parkinson's disease and severe motor symptoms.[1]\n- **Mechanism.** Complication type: hardware complications.[2]\n- **Long-term Outcomes.** The Movement Disorder Society-sponsored revision of the Unified Parkinson's Disease Rating Scale (MDS-UPDRS), validated in a study of 877 patients from 39 sites, demonstrated high internal consistency (Cronbach's alpha = 0.79–0.93 across parts) and strong correlation with the original UPDRS (ρ = 0.96), providing a comprehensive framework for tracking these changes across Efficacy\n\nPopulation: patients with advanced Parkinson's disease and severe motor symptoms.[1] Intervention: this treatment plus medication.[1] Comparator: medication alone.[1] Primary endpoint: the changes from baseline to six months in the quality of life, as assessed by the Parkinson's Disease Questionnaire (PDQ-39), and the severity of symptoms without medication, according to the Unified Parkinson's Disease Rating Scale, part III (UPDRS-III).[1] Timepoint: six months.[1] Study design: randomized-pairs trial.[1]\n\n### Mechanism\n\nComplication type: hardware complications.[2] The Movement Disorder Society-sponsored Unified Parkinson's Disease Rating Scale, known as the MDS-UPDRS, structures the clinical assessment of Parkinson's disease into four distinct and complementary parts.[3] Whereas Part I addresses the non-motor domain, Part II shifts the focus to motor aspects of daily function.[3] However, Part IV specifically records motor complications, distinguishing them from the core motor examination.[3] The combined clinimetric results of this study support the validity of the MDS-UPDRS for rating PD.[3] The primary source identifies hardware complications as a distinct category of DBS-related adverse events.[2] In contrast, the source does not offer guidance on daily life adjustments or support strategies for patients experiencing hardware complications.[2]\n\n### DBS device contraindications and dopaminergic-withdrawal dosing safety\n\nContract-bound content for dbs_device_mri_safety did not survive strict verification against retrieved primary source text; this slot is a curator-actionable gap. See manifest.frame_coverage_report and human_gap_tasks.json for per-entity detail.[4]\n\n### Long-term Outcomes\n\nThe Movement Disorder Society-sponsored revision of the Unified Parkinson's Disease Rating Scale (MDS-UPDRS), validated in a study of 877 patients from 39 sites, demonstrated high internal consistency (Cronbach's alpha = 0.79–0.93 across parts) and strong correlation with the original UPDRS (ρ = 0.96), providing a comprehensive framework for tracking these changes across four parts: non-motor experiences of daily living, motor experiences of daily living, motor examination, and motor complications.[3] In advanced stages, symptoms are severe and daily life usually requires full-time support.[5]\n\n### Safety\n\nThe FDA labeling for carbidopa-levodopa explicitly warns that patients should be observed carefully when the dosage is reduced abruptly or discontinued, especially if they are receiving neuroleptics.[6] DBS hardware complications are relatively common; in a prospective series of 81 consecutive patients with PD undergoing subthalamic nucleus (STN) DBS followed for a mean of 17 months, hardware complications occurred in 26.2% of patients (atom_009).[2] Specific complications included infections requiring system removal in 2.5% (atom_010) and implantable pulse generator (IPG) removal in 3.7% (atom_011), misplaced leads in 12.5% (atom_012), and lead migration, fracture, or IPG malfunction.[2] A review of the FDA MAUDE database over 10 years identified 221 unique adverse events, with infection being the most common (16.2%) (atom_013) and lead migration accounting for 8.6% (atom_014).[7] Over a third (40%) of all devices with adverse events required return to the operating room for explant or revision (atom_003, atom_004).[7] A review of postoperative psychosis after DBS reported a lifetime incidence of psychosis of 28.3% (atom_018) in PD patients following DBS, underscoring the need for vigilance.[8] Additionally, implantable pulse generator expiration can precipitate a movement disorder emergency with severe parkinsonian worsening or akinetic crisis, necessitating urgent medical evaluation.[9] Abrupt reduction or discontinuation of carbidopa-levodopa has been associated with a symptom complex resembling neuroleptic malignant syndrome (NMS), characterized by hyperthermia, muscle rigidity, altered consciousness, and autonomic dysfunction.[6] Psychosis can emerge after STN DBS, as illustrated by clinical cases where patients developed paranoid ideation, hallucinations, and delusions within months of surgery, requiring antipsychotic treatment.[8] Patients taking dopaminergic medications may experience intense urges to gamble, increased sexual urges, binge eating, and other compulsive behaviors, which may resolve with dose reduction or discontinuation[6]\n\n### Population Subgroups\n\nThe choice between rechargeable and non‑rechargeable IPGs was significantly associated with affordability (χ²=19.13, p<0.001), and remote programming capability influenced brand selection.[10] Despite this, a preliminary survey of 17 DBS patients (85% of those approached) found that 76.5% used their patient programmer once per month or less, 17.7% never used it, and the most common function was checking battery life (58.8%).[11] Adaptive DBS shows promise, as one study reported that overall well‑being significantly improved with adaptive stimulation (p=0.007).[12] Caregivers of intermediate PD spent 6.6 hours per day caring, compared to 3.2 hours for early PD, and were less likely to be employed (25.3% vs 42.4%).[13] Personalized DBS programming using machine learning to optimize gait has produced meaningful improvements in walking, addressing a common post‑DBS challenge.[14] Remote patient monitoring devices led 79.9% of participants to agree that they improved mobility, and 48% perceived themselves as safer.[15] However, a meta‑synthesis of wearable device experiences identified barriers including discomfort, emotional distress, and interface difficulties that hinder long‑term adherence.[16] 87.3% of DBS patients used rechargeable IPGs (r-IPGs), 87.7% were satisfied with their device; among those with r-IPGs, 40.6% felt confident handling the device within one week of discharge, 71.4% recharged independently, and the mean recharge interval was 4.3 days.[10] Caregivers of people with advanced PD had significantly higher perceived burden (ZBI 34.5 vs 24.7 for early PD) and were more likely to take medication due to caregiving (34.7% vs 13.8%).[13] Assistive technologies also contribute: a scoping review reported that home automation significantly increased quality of life (p<0.001), and 63.5% of participants used speech‑to‑text functions to cope with tremors.[15]\n\n### Limitations\n\nLimitations: The pipeline's tier distribution reveals a heavy reliance on lower-quality evidence, with only 10% of sources classified as T1 primary studies and a striking 70% falling into the UNKNOWN tier. Six high-severity contradictions were detected, including a 972.4% relative difference in mortality estimates and a 481.0% divergence in hazard ratio, indicating sources disagree sharply on both magnitude and direction of key outcomes. The evidence horizon spans from 2010-01-01 to the present, but the predominance of unclassified sources and sparse representation in tiers T2 (1%), T3 (1%), and T5 (0%) further limit confidence in the synthesized findings.\n\n## Methods\nPre-registered protocol.json (SHA-256 36a59dc009aa949f...).\nCorpus: Serper + Semantic Scholar + OpenAlex live retrieval, augmented by domain backends (clinical: domain_backends(clinical): {'europe_pmc': 27}).\nRetrieval fetch outcome: 478 of 740 candidate sources fetched; 262 failed or timed out.\nGenerator model: deepseek/deepseek-v4-pro (multi-section: outline + 6 parallel sections + strict_verify + regen-on-failure).\nEvaluator model: google/gemma-4-31b-it (different family).\nSources classified using T1-T7 tier taxonomy.\nInclusion / exclusion per clinical template. boundary\n\nLevel 5:\nFull Driving Automation.[1] Standard id: J3016_202104.[1] The SAE J3016_202104 standard defines six levels of driving automation.[1] Level 0 is No Driving Automation.[1] In designation is UN Regulation No. 157 - Automated Lane Keeping Systems (ALKS).[2] The regulation, UN Regulation No. 157, addresses Automated Lane Keeping Systems.[2]\n\n### NHTSA automated-driving-systems policy and crash-reporting order\n\nScope: Entities named in the General Order.[3] Issuing agency: NHTSA.[3] The scope of this order is precisely defined, applying exclusively to entities named in the General Order.[3] However, the reporting obligation is not triggered for every crash involving those entities; it is conditional upon specific temporal and outcome-based criteria.[3] Alternatively, the obligation arises if the crash involved a vulnerable road user being struck.[3] In contrast, a crash that does not meet these consequence thresholds, even if ADS was active within the 30-second window, would not require reporting under the order.[3] The reporting criteria also encompass an air bag deployment.[3] Finally, any individual being transported to a hospital for medical treatment as a result of the crash mandates a report.[3] By comparison, crashes where ADS was not in use within 30 seconds, or where the damage is below the specified property damage threshold, fall outside the mandatory reporting scope.[3]\n\n### Product-liability / negligence doctrine for ADAS manufacturers\n\nDefect categories: manufacturing defects; design
